    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ea7f77c124254b318ee21903da381092">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '12b277e4-6c64-46a8-b7aa-106d31fd6bf7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d32884409" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>A reference system for SIBs</b> based on in-house Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C and commercial hard carbon active material is established, including standardized electrodes and electrolytes. Typical lab set-ups in half- and full-cell configurations are evaluated for electrochemical data reproducibility and suitability. A TRL 4 pouch cell provides practical data on energy/power densities and temperature behavior.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4e4071b2-4c6d-4e64-a3a6-4a8f109a6466/batt202400406-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4e4071b2-4c6d-4e64-a3a6-4a8f109a6466/batt202400406-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cc2a9c5b-675d-46e4-9610-319781f9f063/batt202400406-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4e4071b2-4c6d-4e64-a3a6-4a8f109a6466/batt202400406-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d32884476" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>At the research level, novel active materials for batteries are synthesised on a small scale, fabricated into electrodes and electrochemically characterised using each group's established process due to the lack of standards. Recently, eminent researchers have criticised the implementation of e. g. low active material contents/electrode loadings, the use of research-type battery cell constructions, or the lack of statistically relevant data, resulting in overstated data and thus giving misleading predictions of the key performance indicators of new battery technologies. Here, we report on the establishment of a reference system for the development of sodium-ion batteries. Electrodes are fabricated under relevant conditions using 9.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> self-synthesised Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode active material and 3.6 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> commercially available hard carbon anode active material. It is found that different types of battery cells are more or less suitable for half- and/or full-cell testing, resulting in ir/reproducible or underestimated active material capacities. Furthermore, the influence of electrode overhang, which is relevant for upscaling, is evaluated. The demonstrator cell (TRL 4–5) has been further characterised providing measured data on the power/energy density and thermal behaviour during rate testing up to 15 C and projections are made for its practical limits.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400406-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400406-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a promising technology to complement or even replace state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries. While the anode material of the first commercially available SIBs is expected to be hard carbon,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0001_R_d32884174e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Sci.">1</a></sup></span> there are a number of different options for cathode active materials, e. g. Prussian Blue analogues, layered oxides, and polyanionic materials.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0002_R_d32884174e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - ACS Omega">2</a></sup></span> Due to a similar operating principle, SIB's advantage lies in a “drop-in” approach for battery cell manufacturing steps at the electrode level, such as slurry mixing, coating, drying, calendering, as well as at the cell assembly level, taking into account electrode winding/stacking, electrolyte filling and cell formation.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0003_R_d32884174e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nanotechnol. Sci. Appl.">3</a></sup></span> However, the viscosity of the slurry changes due to the change in the active material, which affects the coating process and can lead to different designs of the coating equipment and process parameters (e. g. production speed, film thickness). Furthermore, the influence of binder migration during drying varies in its intensity.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0004_R_d32884174e699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Batteries Supercaps">4</a></sup></span> With the same electrode composition, it was found that a slurry containing hard carbon and a water-based binder system (CMC/SBR) leads to less binder migration during rapid drying compared to a standard LIB graphite anode.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0005_R_d32884174e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Tech.">5</a></sup></span> Another example is the use of nanoporous structured particles and the binder PVDF, which results in lower adhesion strength of the electrodes compared to electrodes containing compact particles. The advantage, however, is that the electrochemical performance is not impaired during fast drying.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0006_R_d32884174e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Due to the open porosity of the particles, the binder is distributed over the electrode cross-section and does not clog the pores between the particles. The gravimetric energy density of SIBs has been announced to reach target values of 160 Wh/kg at cell level, which is in the range of LIBs using graphite (Gr) as anode material and lithium iron phosphate (LFP) as the cathode material. Therefore, SIBs are often compared to LFP/Gr batteries and could replace some of the applications of LFP/Gr batteries.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0009_R_d32884174e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a></sup></span> However, there are some differences when comparing LFP/Gr chemistry to SIBs, but the details depend on the actual cathode active material being used. In general, SIBs promise faster charging.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0010_R_d32884174e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span> Further, they are free of critical metals, such as lithium and cobalt. Of course, they are also free of copper, as aluminium can be used as the current collector material for the cathode and anode, which significantly improves their recyclability.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0011_R_d32884174e715" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> Because of the latter fact, they can even be deeply discharged and short-circuited for much safer transport conditions, but this is limited to serval electrolyte and cathode compositions of the cell.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0012_R_d32884174e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a></sup></span> Lastly, they offer greater supply independence because they are free of graphite, which is mostly mined in China.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0013_R_d32884174e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>The above-mentioned arguments for SIBs sound exciting (sometimes in theory). However, for the proof of concept of any functional new battery technology both, cathode and anode active materials combined in a multi-layer electrode full-cell setup must demonstrate sufficient performance under realistic conditions. More specifically, a high active material content and high electrode loadings, as well as proper balancing conditions together with a limited amount of charge carriers originating from the cathode and electrolyte reservoir need to be set to allow for evaluation of energy/power densities and cycle life, temperature behaviour, etc. at the battery cell level. In fact, recent papers have highlighted the importance of putting performance data of newly developed active battery materials from basic research into perspective. Frith et al. criticised the over-extrapolation of early research results as they are “free of several limitations that govern practical applications”.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0017_R_d32884174e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span> Amine and co-workers summarised a number of crucial test parameters that in their opinion are “often overlooked in academic literature but [that] are critical for practical applicability outside the laboratory” in their publication “Bridging the academic and industrial metrics for next generation practical batteries”.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0018_R_d32884174e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">13</a></sup></span> Johansson et al. humorously listed the ‘Ten Ways to Fool the Masses When Presenting Battery Research’, also highlighting the importance of proper material characterisation, and technologically relevant active material loadings.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0019_R_d32884174e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Batteries Supercaps">14</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, in addition to missing key performance indicators (KPIs) for evaluating new active materials for batteries, there is also a lack of standardisation of test conditions, which has led to discussions within the research community.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0020_R_d32884174e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">15</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To address these issues, we decided to establish a reference system of well-defined active materials, electrodes and electrolytes for our common SIB research topics, but also to showcase a best practice SIB reference system. In order to increase the benefit to the research community, reference materials have been distributed to 17 research groups and some OEM's across Europe. This work is therefore not an isolated study, but rather a starting point, including a basic characterisation of the benchmark materials, which can serve as a reference for future studies on these materials. For the anode material, we defined and used hard carbon from Kuraray as a benchmark material. On the cathode side, the lack of satisfactory commercial sources for active material motivated us to synthesise our own cathode material, namely carbon-coated sodium vanadium phosphate (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C). The stable cycling behaviour of this SIB chemistry was a key reason why we decided to set the aforementioned active materials as a standard and benchmark system. In a previous study, after cell formation, discharge capacities of up to 92.6 mAh/g were achieved, and capacity retention &gt;90 % over 1000 charge/discharge cycles at 0.5 C/0.5 C could be reached.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0021_R_d32884174e748" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Batteries Supercaps">16</a></sup></span> The reference electrodes were prepared according to the following KPIs, which are critical for application-relevant performance testing: both the cathode and the anode active material loadings were close to commercial standards, &gt;90 wt % active material loadings were realised with 3.6 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> hard carbon (~1.2 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup>) and 9.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> NVP/C (~1.0 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup>). For full-cells, the balancing of the negative/positive capacity ratio (N/P ratio) has been adjusted for initial loss of sodium-ions during formation and was kept as close to 1 as possible to maximise the total energy density. However, the total capacity of the hard carbon electrode slightly exceeds that of the NVP/C electrode to avoid any sodium plating.</p>
         
         <p>In the second part of this study, we present the results of a round robin study on the reference electrodes, where four different types of laboratory cell formats are compared. Using different types of half-cells, either the cathode or anode is installed against elemental sodium to obtain individual readings for potential curves, specific ir/reversible capacities, and initial/following coulombic efficiencies (ICE/CE) of the respective electrodes. Coin cells or similar commercially available two- or three-electrode cell configurations were used for this purpose. This was motivated by the fact that reliable electrode capacity measurements are the key to correctly adjust the balancing of the full-cell. As recently demonstrated, it is surprisingly challenging to obtain reproducible and correct data from half-cells measurements against elemental sodium, especially with hard carbon electrodes.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0022_R_d32884174e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Batteries Supercaps">17</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>As degradation/aging may be caused by different factors in SIB systems compared to our recent LIB study,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0023_R_d32884174e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Batteries Supercaps">18</a></sup></span> we were keen not only to compare the electrochemical data obtained in (different types of) sodium-ion half-cells, but also to examine and contrast the results obtained from coin cells, single-layer and multi-layer pouch cells to one another in full-cell configuration. Finally, pilot-level battery cells, namely single-layer (~19 mAh after formation) and larger footprint area multi-layer pouch cells (~3.5 Ah after formation) are used to validate full-cell characteristics, such as ICE/CE and initial cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400406-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400406-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400406-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400406-sec-0003-title"> NVP/C Active Material Specifications</h3>
            
            <p>From the synthesis described in the experimental section, a total of 2.5 kg of the NVP/C cathode active material was synthesised. SEM images of the active material are shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400406-fig-0001">1</a>. As reported previously<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0024_R_d32884174e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span> NVP/C is a composite material consisting of carbon-coated nano-crystalline NVP primary particles. As a result of the spray-drying synthesis and calcination, the particles are agglomerated in the form of porous spherical granules, as shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400406-fig-0001">1</a>. The corresponding pore size distribution is shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400406-fig-0002">2a</a>. In accordance with the cross-sectional SEM images of the material, pore diameters of &lt;300 nm were attributed to pores inside granules. The corresponding cumulative pore volume of 278 mm<sup>3</sup>/g, yields an internal porosity of 44.9 %, based on the density of the NVP/C active material of 2.93 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, which was determined by means of He-gas pycnometry. The specific surface area of the material was determined to be 96.5 m<sup>2</sup>/g.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202400406-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b1a9489a-e3f2-4850-825c-a35791908487/batt202400406-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b1a9489a-e3f2-4850-825c-a35791908487/batt202400406-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/15731022-2216-4724-8779-e8482497196a/batt202400406-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b1a9489a-e3f2-4850-825c-a35791908487/batt202400406-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400406-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400406" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>SEM images of the NVP/C active material (top). SEM images of cross-sections of NVP/C (bottom), revealing the porous structure of granules which consist of NVP primary particles embedded in a carbon matrix.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202400406-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/56b0674c-560d-4a20-9ee1-4c2dbb08d38b/batt202400406-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/56b0674c-560d-4a20-9ee1-4c2dbb08d38b/batt202400406-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cd097870-5bc7-4aa7-b35e-bf32180f90b1/batt202400406-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/56b0674c-560d-4a20-9ee1-4c2dbb08d38b/batt202400406-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400406-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400406" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>a) Size distribution of nano-pores inside the NVP/C granules (determined by mercury intrusion porosimetry). b) Volumetric particle size distribution for the NVP/C granules (determined by laser scattering).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The secondary particle size distribution of the active material was determined by laser scattering and is shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400406-fig-0002">2b</a>. The spherical granules obtained from the final calcination process have diameters ranging from 7.3 μm (<i>D</i><sub>10</sub>) to 17.0 μm (<i>D</i><sub>90</sub>) with an average value of 11.9 μm. The carbon content of the active material was determined to be 11.2 wt % by means of elemental analysis. For NVP, the stoichiometry Na<sub>3.00</sub>V<sub>1.99</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3.01</sub> is found, which confirms the ideal composition within the scope of the measurement accuracy. A table with detailed analysis results can be found in the Table S1 in the supporting information (SI).</p>
            
            <p>The X-ray diffraction pattern together with the Rietveld refinement results of the synthesised NVP/C composite cathode material are shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400406-fig-0003">3</a>. The average crystal structure of the NVP phase can be described by the rhombohedral <i>R</i><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/387948d9-8369-48bd-8fed-b5a863c27d32/batt202400406-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
               <i>c</i> NaSICON-type structure,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0025_R_d32884174e893" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">20</a></sup></span> with refined lattice parameters of <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=872.73(2) pm and <i>c</i>=2186.14(8) pm. The estimated primary crystallite size calculated from a double-Voigt approach as implemented in the TOPAS software is 121(3) nm.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0026_R_d32884174e903" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">21</a></sup></span> In addition to the main NVP phase, small amounts of tetragonal zirconium dioxide (ZrO<sub>2</sub>) and vanadium carbide (VC) are also present in the synthesised composite. Based on the Rietveld refinements the weight fraction of ZrO<sub>2</sub> is 1.0 % and that of VC is 0.7 %. The ZrO<sub>2</sub> originates from the grinding debris of the used ZrO<sub>2</sub> milling balls used and the VC originates from the synthesis process as we have described in a previous publication.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0027_R_d32884174e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ChemElectroChem">22</a></sup></span> Despite comparison with common databases like ICDD-PDF, COD and ICSD, we were not able to find any phases that match the weak reflections at 13.6°, 16.1° and 22.6°. However, based on the weak diffracted intensities, we estimate the phase fraction of the unidentified impurity below 1 wt %, so the influence on the calculated capacity should be minimal.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="batt202400406-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5bd12554-a6a2-4aa2-a5f1-4ab89d1cf7be/batt202400406-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5bd12554-a6a2-4aa2-a5f1-4ab89d1cf7be/batt202400406-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1c3b20c7-c906-4071-84fb-d5aeec11c01e/batt202400406-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5bd12554-a6a2-4aa2-a5f1-4ab89d1cf7be/batt202400406-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=batt202400406-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fbatt.202400406" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Powder X-Ray diffraction pattern and Rietveld refinement of the NVP/C cathode active material. For the sake of clarity, the background and individual phase contributions have been set off vertically by 15 counts. Reflections marked with an asterisk could not be assigned.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The theoretical capacity of pure NVP corresponding to the V<sup>IV</sup>/V<sup>III</sup> redox couple occurring at a potential of ~3.4 (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) according to Equation (1) is 117.6 mAh/g (NVP).
               <div class="inline-equation" id="batt202400406-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/60595f76-7b5b-40c1-9234-6c954d413361/batt202400406-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>However, this capacity cannot be achieved on material level, since the composite material contains 11.2 wt % carbon and secondary phases (~2 wt %). Taking these electrochemically inactive components into account, a capacity of 102.1 mAh/g is expected for the NVP/C active material investigated herein. Despite the fact that NVP/C is not a perfectly phase-pure material, the advantages of using it as a reference material for research are obvious: NVP/C has good electrochemical properties and shows excellent cycling stability,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0021_R_d32884174e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Batteries Supercaps">16</a></sup></span> but more importantly, it is possible to produce this material with reproducible properties on a kg scale.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400406-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400406-sec-0004-title"> Hard Carbon Active Material Specifications</h3>
            
            <p>As anode active material, commercial hard carbon (HC), namely Kuranode Type II, 9 μm (Kuraray) was used. According to the material data sheet,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0028_R_d32884174e1064" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> the first charge capacity of the material is 332 mAh/g (0.1 mA/cm<sup>2</sup> constant current (CC), with a constant voltage (CV) phase at 0 V until I &lt;0.02 mA/cm<sup>2</sup>). Note that the electrode loading is not specified and thus it is not possible to calculate the C-rate, which was used to determine this capacity. The 1<sup>st</sup> discharge capacity is stated to be 298 mAh/g. The irreversible capacity loss is reported to be 34 mAh/g corresponding to an ICE of 89.8 %. The charge capacity could be confirmed experimentally previously<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0022_R_d32884174e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Batteries Supercaps">17</a></sup></span> and in the present study. The discharge capacity and thus the ICE value could not be fully reproduced, which however will be discussed in detail below.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400406-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400406-sec-0005-title"> Electrolyte Specifications</h3>
            
            <p>As listed in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202400406-tbl-0001">1</a>, electrolytes based on a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) with the conductive salt sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>), and, in the case of the half-cells, the additive fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) were used for the investigation of the HC and NVP/C electrodes. The electrolytes were selected based on the results of a previous electrolyte screening<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0022_R_d32884174e1090" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Batteries Supercaps">17</a></sup></span> and, in the case of the full-cell electrolyte without FEC, based on the proven long-term cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0021_R_d32884174e1093" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Batteries Supercaps">16</a></sup></span> Our selection is confirmed by more recent studies, which likewise conclude that FEC is not a suitable additive for NVP vs. HC full-cells.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400406-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400406-bib-0029_R_d32884174e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res.">24</a></sup></span> The water content of the electrolyte B was determined to be below 20 ppm by means of Karl-Fischer titration. Details of the electrolyte preparation are provided in the experimental section.
</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="batt202400406-tbl-0001">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400406-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400406-sec-0006-title"> Electrode Properties</h3>
            
            <p>The often drastic differences between electrode formulations and loadings in research papers compared to technologically relevant electrodes were already addressed in the introduction. Results obtained from ultrathin research electrodes cannot easily be extrapolated to the properties of electrodes with technologically relevant active material loadings. To tackle this issue, and to create a reliable basis for realistic projections, areal capacities of 1.0 and 1.2 mAh/cm<sup>2</sup> for the cathode and anode, respectively, were defined as target values for the reference electrodes. As shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202400406-tbl-0002">2</a>, and as discussed in the later section of the round robin studies, these values were achieved in good approximation.
</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="batt202400406-tbl-0002">
               
               
               
               
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  2.

Experimental capacities are discussed below.</header>
